Thought for 16s
Yes — this is the right move.

For something this ambitious, the cleanest setup is a prompt package with each doc handling a distinct concern at full depth, while all of them still align to one product truth.

Use them in this order:

Master orchestration prompt

Domain, schema, and system contracts prompt

Backend, services, and workflow engine prompt

Frontend, mobile app, and UX prompt

AI, ops, memory, and autonomy prompt

Integrations, notifications, deployment, testing, and docs prompt

Important usage rule for the builder:

load Prompt 1 first

then use the others as binding implementation docs

the later prompts must not override the master product intent unless explicitly stated

if there is conflict, the priority order is: Prompt 1 → Prompt 2 → Prompt 3 → Prompt 4 → Prompt 5 → Prompt 6

Below is the full package.

Prompt 1 — Master Orchestration Prompt
You are building a production-minded, AI-native, messaging-first booking platform for service businesses.

The first launch vertical is barbers, but the architecture must be generic enough to later support:

nail techs

lash techs

makeup artists

tutors

personal trainers

tattoo artists

mechanics

mobile beauty professionals

home service providers

other service businesses that rely on scheduled appointments

This is not just a barber app. It is a platform that begins with barbers.

The core product thesis is:

Customers should be able to book where they already communicate, while the business manages everything in one place.

The product should feel like:

“The booking system that lives in your DMs.”

The product must launch as:

a mobile app on iOS

a mobile app on Android

a web-based public booking experience

a web-admin/business management experience

a platform that is architected from day one for messaging-channel expansion and AI-native operations

Product goals
The platform must allow customers to book through:

mobile app

public web booking page

WhatsApp

Instagram DMs

SMS

Snapchat identity / login

future channels later

The platform must unify those interactions into:

one customer identity

one customer history

one booking history

one operational view for the business

The business owner must be able to:

create and manage their business

manage staff

manage services

manage availability

manage blocked time and time off

manage bookings

view customer history

receive notifications

view AI observations and operational summaries

later approve or deny AI-recommended actions

Core product philosophy

Do not hardcode barber-specific assumptions into architecture, schema, naming, routes, services, or UI.

Use generic domain terms such as:

Prompt 2 — Domain Model, Schema, State Rules, and System Contracts
Implement the system domain in a rigorous, explicit way. This prompt defines the canonical domain model, schema expectations, state models, entity relationships, and cross-cutting contracts.

1. Domain architecture
Treat the application as a multi-tenant business platform.

A business is the primary tenant.
All staff, services, customers, bookings, rules, notifications, AI observations, incidents, and related records belong directly or indirectly to a business.

Do not create ambiguous ownership.

2. Canonical entities
Implement these entities with clear relations, indexes, enums, and constraints.

User
Represents an authenticated app user.
Normally:

business owner

staff member with app access

future admin

Fields:

id

email

fullName nullable

role enum

authProvider nullable

createdAt

updatedAt

Role enum:

BUSINESS_OWNER

STAFF

PLATFORM_ADMIN

Rules:

one user can own one or more businesses if multi-business support is later desired; architect sensibly

for MVP, optimize for one owner managing one business, but do not make schema impossible to extend

Business
Fields:

id

ownerId

name

slug

description nullable

email nullable

phone nullable

addressLine1 nullable

addressLine2 nullable

city nullable

state nullable

postalCode nullable

country nullable

timezone

logoUrl nullable

coverImageUrl nullable

isActive boolean

createdAt

updatedAt

Constraints:

slug unique globally

ownerId indexed

timezone required

soft-deactivation path should be possible

Staff
Fields:

id

businessId

userId nullable

firstName

lastName nullable

displayName

email nullable

phone nullable

photoUrl nullable

bio nullable

isActive boolean

createdAt

updatedAt

Rules:

a staff record may exist without a linked user account

a staff member may later get app access

all staff belong to exactly one business

a business may nominate one other staff as second/backup admin but that staff should not have same full access as the primary staff so build out that workflow sensibly 

Service
Fields:

id

businessId

name

description nullable

category nullable

durationMinutes

bufferBeforeMinutes default 0

bufferAfterMinutes default 0

priceMinor

currency

imageUrl nullable

isActive boolean

createdAt

updatedAt

Rules:

priceMinor is stored as integer minor units

durationMinutes must be positive

service can be soft-disabled

service belongs to a business

StaffServiceAssignment
Create an explicit join table if needed so staff-to-service assignment is clean.

Fields:

id

businessId

staffId

serviceId

createdAt

Constraint:

unique on staffId + serviceId

Customer
Fields:

id

businessId

firstName nullable

lastName nullable

displayName nullable

email nullable

phone nullable

notes nullable

tags Json nullable

isBlocked boolean default false

createdAt

updatedAt

Rules:

customers are scoped to a business

the same human across businesses does not need to be globally unified yet

within a business, identity merging should be possible through ChannelIdentity

ChannelIdentity
Fields:

id

customerId

channelType enum

externalId

username nullable

displayName nullable

metadata Json nullable

createdAt

updatedAt

ChannelType enum:

WEB

APP

WHATSAPP

SMS

INSTAGRAM

SNAPCHAT

EMAIL

Constraints:

unique on customerId + channelType + externalId

index externalId

index channelType

Rules:

multiple identities may point to the same customer

this is the core identity-unification mechanism

Booking
Fields:

id

businessId

staffId nullable

serviceId

customerId

channelType enum

startAt

endAt

status enum

notes nullable

internalNotes nullable

cancellationReason nullable

createdAt

updatedAt

BookingStatus enum:

PENDING

CONFIRMED

CANCELLED

COMPLETED

NO_SHOW

Rules:

bookings always belong to a business

bookings always belong to one service and one customer

staff may be optional if the business model later allows auto-assignment, but current booking logic must not create ambiguous invalid bookings

endAt must be computed reliably from service duration plus optional business logic

booking conflict checks must be deterministic

AvailabilityRule
Fields:

id

businessId nullable

staffId nullable

dayOfWeek int

startTime

endTime

isActive boolean

createdAt

updatedAt

Rules:

dayOfWeek should be 0–6 or 1–7, but choose one convention and use it everywhere

either businessId or staffId must be present

if both are allowed, document semantics carefully

staff availability must never silently bypass business availability

TimeOff
Fields:

3. Booking-state rules
The booking lifecycle is strict.

Allowed flows:

PENDING → CONFIRMED

PENDING → CANCELLED

CONFIRMED → COMPLETED

CONFIRMED → CANCELLED

CONFIRMED → NO_SHOW

Disallow nonsensical transitions unless explicitly designed and documented.

Examples:

CANCELLED should not become COMPLETED

NO_SHOW should not become CONFIRMED without a deliberate admin override workflow

4. Availability rules
Availability is composed from:

business-level working hours

staff-level working hours

blocked time

time off

booking duration

booking buffers

slot interval

timezone

Slot generation rules:

do not show slots outside business hours

do not show slots outside staff hours

do not show slots overlapping time off

do not show slots that conflict with existing bookings

do not show slots that cannot fit full duration plus buffers

do not show past slots

use timezone-aware calculations consistently

Default slot interval:

15 minutes

5. Identity merge rules
When future messaging integrations are used, customer identity resolution must follow deterministic rules.

Priority examples:

if matching ChannelIdentity exists, use linked customer

else if strong unique match exists on known phone/email within business scope, candidate merge may be suggested

else create new customer safely

Do not silently merge weak matches unless rule is explicit.

6. Event taxonomy requirements
Implement strong event naming discipline.

Core events include:

USER_SIGNED_UP

USER_SIGNED_IN

USER_SIGNED_OUT

BUSINESS_CREATED

BUSINESS_UPDATED

STAFF_CREATED

STAFF_UPDATED

STAFF_DEACTIVATED

SERVICE_CREATED

SERVICE_UPDATED

SERVICE_DEACTIVATED

AVAILABILITY_UPDATED

TIME_OFF_CREATED

TIME_OFF_REMOVED

BOOKING_ATTEMPTED

BOOKING_CREATED

BOOKING_FAILED

BOOKING_CANCELLED

BOOKING_COMPLETED

BOOKING_NO_SHOW

CUSTOMER_CREATED

CUSTOMER_UPDATED

NOTIFICATION_SENT

NOTIFICATION_FAILED

DEVICE_TOKEN_REGISTERED

AI_REQUESTED

AI_RESPONSE

AI_ERROR

AI_OBSERVATION_CREATED

INCIDENT_CREATED

INCIDENT_UPDATED

REMEDIATION_ACTION_CREATED

KNOWLEDGE_ENTRY_CREATED

7. Constraints and indexes
Use explicit indexes for:

businessId on all major business-scoped records

staffId on bookings

customerId on bookings

startAt on bookings

slug on business

externalId on channel identity

createdAt where sensible for observation, event, message, incident history

8. Seed data requirements
Seed data must include:

one demo business

at least three staffs

at least four services

realistic business hours

realistic staff schedules

at least six bookings across mixed states

at least five customers

at least one AI observation

at least one incident

at least one remediation action

at least one knowledge entry

Requirements:

conflict-safe logic

keep deterministic validation primary

17. Business logic non-negotiables

Prompt 5 — AI, Product Intelligence, Operational Intelligence, Knowledge, Memory, Observations, Incidents, and Autonomy Policy

This prompt defines the AI-native core of the platform. Build this with depth, rigor, and clear long-term intent.


The platform is intended to become progressively more self-observing, self-understanding, and lower-touch over time. That means AI must be designed into the architecture correctly from day one, not bolted on later as a chatbot or reporting feature.


AI in this platform is not a cosmetic assistant. It is a system-wide intelligence layer that helps the product understand customers, helps operations understand what is happening, and helps the platform preserve and apply knowledge over time.



1. AI mission in this platform

The AI layer serves two primary intelligence domains:


Product intelligence

Purpose:


improve booking experience

understand customer behavior

support future conversational booking

support business insight generation

support recommendations such as rebooking, service suggestions, and no-show risk

help the platform become more personalized, predictive, and context-aware over time


Operational intelligence

Purpose:


observe logs, events, workflows, and system signals

detect issues and abnormal patterns

summarize incidents and important changes

recommend low-risk next steps

preserve useful operational knowledge over time

reduce manual operational overhead

help the platform become increasingly self-observing and easier to run


These two domains are powered by a shared Knowledge, Memory, and Context layer.


This shared layer is what allows the system to understand not only what users are doing and what the platform is experiencing, but also what the business means, what rules apply, what has happened before, and what should happen next.


AI is therefore not only a chatbot. It is an intelligence architecture spanning product behavior, operational awareness, institutional knowledge, and future safe autonomy.



2. Core AI framing

The correct conceptual model is:


Product Intelligence = understands customer behavior, booking behavior, preferences, patterns, and product-side recommendations

Operational Intelligence = understands platform health, incidents, anomalies, reliability, workflows, and operational suggestions

Knowledge, Memory, and Context Layer = stores and retrieves the rules, policies, histories, decisions, patterns, docs, and reusable understanding that support both domains


Do not introduce a separate third “business intelligence” domain unless later phases explicitly add forecasting, profitability optimization, branch strategy, pricing strategy, or competitive intelligence.


For this stage, business understanding should exist primarily as a shared knowledge and memory substrate, not as a separate intelligence pillar.



3. Knowledge scope — what the system should be able to understand

The system must be architected to understand business context at two levels:


Platform-level knowledge

This is knowledge about how the platform itself works globally.


Examples:


booking lifecycle rules

membership logic

pricing and deposit logic

refund and cancellation frameworks

product definitions

role permissions

feature behavior

admin workflows

support playbooks

incident history

release notes

internal operational guidance

architectural context

platform-level business rules


Tenant-level knowledge

This is knowledge about how each tenant business operates locally within the platform.


Examples:


tenant cancellation policies

tenant refund rules

staff lateness handling

tenant service definitions

tenant-specific membership rules

commission models

VIP handling rules

complaint handling preferences

opening and closing procedures

walk-in policy

branch-specific rules

tenant-specific operating patterns

manager override behavior

frequently repeated exceptions

local terminology and service naming


This distinction is important.


The platform must eventually be able to reason using both:


global platform rules

tenant-specific operating rules


This allows the system to behave intelligently in context instead of treating every business identically.



4. AI design requirements

The AI layer must be:


centralized

modular

auditable

observable

safe by default

task-specific

resilient to provider failures

explainable where practical

capable of future expansion without architectural rewrite

grounded in stored knowledge and prior outcomes rather than stateless prompts only


All AI calls must go through one shared infrastructure path.


No AI module should directly call a model provider ad hoc.



5. Required AI module architecture

Implement these folders and modules.


Shared AI infrastructure

src/ai/shared/aiClient.ts

src/ai/shared/aiLogging.ts

src/ai/shared/aiPolicies.ts

src/ai/shared/promptTemplates.ts

src/ai/shared/aiTypes.ts

src/ai/shared/aiGuards.ts

src/ai/shared/contextBuilder.ts

src/ai/shared/knowledgeResolver.ts

src/ai/shared/memoryResolver.ts


Responsibilities:


provider client setup

consistent callAI() wrapper

metadata capture

retries and timeouts

logging to AIInteraction and Event

structured task definitions

policy checks

safe failure handling

room for future model selection

retrieval of relevant context before generation

assembly of platform-level and tenant-level knowledge context

memory-aware execution support


Product AI

src/ai/product/bookingAssistant.ts

src/ai/product/customerInsights.ts

src/ai/product/riskScoring.ts

src/ai/product/rebookingAssistant.ts

src/ai/product/serviceSuggestion.ts


Ops AI

src/ai/ops/opsMonitor.ts

src/ai/ops/logAnalyzer.ts

src/ai/ops/incidentSummarizer.ts

src/ai/ops/anomalyDetector.ts

src/ai/ops/remediationAdvisor.ts

src/ai/ops/knowledgeSynthesizer.ts


Knowledge and memory modules

src/ai/knowledge/knowledgeIngestor.ts

src/ai/knowledge/knowledgeClassifier.ts

src/ai/knowledge/policyExtractor.ts

src/ai/knowledge/tenantKnowledgeManager.ts

src/ai/memory/memoryRecorder.ts

src/ai/memory/memoryLinker.ts

src/ai/memory/feedbackRecorder.ts


These modules are not a third intelligence domain. They are foundational infrastructure supporting both Product and Operational Intelligence.



6. Shared AI client contract

Implement a standardized AI call contract.


Input should support:


module name

purpose

task type

businessId optional

userId optional

entityType optional

entityId optional

tenantId optional

input payload

retrieved context bundle

expected structured output shape if applicable


Output should support:


success or failure

text output or structured output

confidence if available

rationale if applicable

latency

error summary if failed

context sources used

policy decision outcome where relevant


Every call must:


write AIInteraction

emit AI_REQUESTED and AI_RESPONSE or AI_ERROR

record context inputs used

never silently fail



7. Product AI modules — explicit expectations

bookingAssistant

Purpose:


future conversational interpretation for booking

suggest likely services

suggest likely times

help parse natural language requests

use customer context and business context where appropriate


For now:


implement as callable module with defined input and output types

do not make it the source of truth for booking validation

do not let free-form AI override deterministic booking rules


customerInsights

Purpose:


summarize customer behavior

identify frequent patterns

note likely preferences

provide concise insight cards for owner view


Input examples:


customer history

booking patterns

no-show history

channel usage

service preferences

branch patterns


Output examples:


summary paragraph

top behavioral cues

suggested next action


riskScoring

Purpose:


estimate no-show risk

estimate customer reliability

later influence deposit suggestions


For now:


use hybrid logic

deterministic factors should be primary where possible

AI may enrich explanation, not make opaque final decisions blindly


rebookingAssistant

Purpose:


identify likely rebooking windows

suggest customers who are due for another visit

later support reminder targeting


serviceSuggestion

Purpose:


infer likely service fit from limited context

later help conversational bookings choose likely matching services



8. Ops AI modules — explicit expectations

opsMonitor

Purpose:


aggregate operational signals

create summaries of current platform health

identify where deeper inspection is needed


logAnalyzer

Purpose:


inspect structured events and errors

cluster related failures

identify repeating failure patterns


incidentSummarizer

Purpose:


take event clusters or issue data and generate concise incident summaries

produce readable summaries for founder or operator review


anomalyDetector

Purpose:


compare current metrics or event patterns against normal patterns

identify unusual spikes such as:

booking failures

notification failures

route errors

sudden drop in usage

unusual refund spikes

unusual cancellation behavior


remediationAdvisor

Purpose:


suggest safe next steps for an issue

classify suggestions by risk and approval need


knowledgeSynthesizer

Purpose:


convert repeated patterns and resolved incidents into reusable knowledge entries

identify when repeated operator behavior should become formalized guidance



9. Knowledge and context system requirements

The platform must include a first-class Knowledge Layer that stores reusable understanding for both product and ops use cases.


Knowledge entries may come from:


manually defined platform rules

tenant configuration

uploaded documents

extracted policies

repeated operational patterns

resolved incidents

accepted AI recommendations

observed tenant behavior that has been reviewed or formalized

admin-authored guidance

release or change notes

support or operational playbooks


Knowledge should be categorized and reviewable.


Core categories may include:


POLICY

SOP

CONFIG_RULE

INCIDENT_LESSON

PRODUCT_GUIDANCE

TENANT_RULE

PLATFORM_RULE

STAFF_WORKFLOW

CUSTOMER_PATTERN

OPERATIONAL_PATTERN

EXCEPTION_RULE


The knowledge layer should later support:


retrieval for incident advice

retrieval for AI recommendation context

retrieval for product recommendations

retrieval for operator decision support

retrieval for tenant-specific context

retrieval for future conversational admin assistance



10. Tenant knowledge capture policy

Tenant knowledge must be designed for progressive capture, not heavy up-front onboarding.


Do not design tenant onboarding as a long questionnaire requiring the business to fully describe itself before it can start using the platform.


Instead, use a layered strategy:


Minimum viable onboarding capture

Only collect what is required to get a tenant live quickly.


Examples:


business name

branch or location basics

opening hours

staff members

services

durations

pricing

booking availability

basic cancellation rule

basic refund rule

payment or deposit preference


Progressive knowledge capture after onboarding

The rest of tenant understanding should be built over time through:


structured settings updates

optional admin setup screens

document uploads

contextual questions

operational observations

repeated manager actions

exception handling patterns

feedback loops


Conversational and contextual capture

The system may ask targeted questions only when relevant.


Examples:


“How should future no-shows be handled?”

“Would you like to formalize this refund decision as a rule?”

“Managers have manually approved this exception several times. Save as policy?”

“There is no lateness policy defined. Would you like to create one?”


Observed-behavior capture

The platform should be able to detect repeated patterns such as:


repeated manual overrides

repeated refund exceptions

recurring VIP treatment

consistent manager assignment preferences

repeated premium-service routing choices

repeated complaint handling paths


The system may surface these as suggested rules, but should not silently assume them as truth without review where business risk exists.


Document ingestion support

Architect for future ingestion of:


PDFs

docs

handbooks

SOPs

policy sheets

spreadsheets

training materials

service lists

internal notes


The system should later be able to extract rules, summarize content, identify contradictions, and convert unstructured material into structured knowledge.



11. AI memory model

The system should “learn from itself” by storing the right artifacts, not by pretending to have magical self-training from day one.


Capture and preserve:


AI interactions

AI observations

incidents

remediation actions

knowledge entries

booking success and failure patterns

owner approval or rejection of suggestions

outcomes of AI suggestions

tenant rule definitions

repeated operator behaviors

repeated tenant exception patterns

useful context attached to incidents and decisions


The minimum lifecycle is:


event occurs

AI or rule creates observation

observation may escalate to incident

incident may lead to remediation proposal

remediation result is stored

repeated resolved patterns become knowledge

accepted knowledge may later influence future AI context and recommendations



12. Observation system requirements

AIObservation is a first-class system object.


Observations can come from:


AI module analysis

deterministic anomaly rules

mixed signal detection

tenant behavior pattern detection

repeated operational actions

repeated product-side behavior


Examples:


“Booking failures spiked over the last 30 minutes”

“Customer tends to book every 21 days and is overdue”

“Reminder send failures increased after the last config change”

“Service X has unusually high abandonment in booking flow”

“Managers repeatedly waive late cancellation penalties for repeat customers”

“Premium services are consistently reassigned to a small subset of staff”


Observation fields must be meaningful enough to power:


AI insights UI

escalation into incidents

later remediation suggestions

tenant rule suggestions

future knowledge synthesis



13. Incident system requirements

Incidents represent more serious operational or system-level issues.


Typical incident categories:


BOOKING_FAILURE

NOTIFICATION_FAILURE

AUTH_FAILURE

DEPLOYMENT_REGRESSION

INTEGRATION_FAILURE

DATA_CONSISTENCY_RISK

AVAILABILITY_ANOMALY

CONFIGURATION_RISK

POLICY_CONFLICT_RISK


Incident flow:


created

investigated

mitigated

resolved

closed


The system must make it possible to:


open incidents

view incidents

attach context

attach remediation actions

attach observations

later summarize incidents

link incidents to resulting knowledge entries where appropriate



14. Remediation action model

RemediationAction must support:


proposal

approval state

execution state

result summary

who initiated it

whether AI proposed it

whether approval is required

linked incident or observation

linked policy category


Action categories may include:


RECOMMEND_RETRY

RECOMMEND_ROLLBACK

RECOMMEND_DEGRADED_MODE

RECOMMEND_DISABLE_FEATURE

RECOMMEND_REQUIRE_DEPOSIT

RECOMMEND_NOTIFY_OWNER

RECOMMEND_FORMALIZE_POLICY

FUTURE_EXECUTE_LOW_RISK_RETRY


For MVP:


focus on proposal and traceability

not dangerous automatic execution



15. AI policy and autonomy boundaries

Implement explicit policy thinking.


AI is allowed now

summarization

classification

insight generation

suggestion generation

observation creation

incident summary drafting

low-risk recommendation drafting

contextual knowledge retrieval

rule suggestion drafting

policy formalization suggestions for human review


AI is not allowed now

destructive database changes

automatic deletion

code changes

automatic repricing

silent customer communications with business risk

automatic disabling of critical product features without policy

silently creating or enforcing risky tenant policies without review


AI may be allowed later with policy

low-risk feature flag toggles

degraded mode activation

retrying safe workflows

escalation and alerting

suggesting deposit rules

low-risk tenant workflow automation

approved rule application in well-bounded cases


Model actions using policy categories:


RECOMMENDATION_ONLY

REQUIRES_APPROVAL

AUTO_ALLOWED_LOW_RISK

BLOCKED



16. AI outputs must be structured

For each AI module, define:


input type

output type

expected fields

confidence or rationale if helpful

safe fallback on failure


Prefer structured parseable outputs over vague prose whenever possible.

summary

recommendedAction

likelyCause

confidence



17. AI failure handling

If AI is unavailable:

dashboard must still work

AI failures must be logged

knowledge retrieval failures must not break core workflows

These views should make AI visible, traceable, and understandable.



19. AI-driven jobs and workflows

Architect for future periodic jobs like:

Do not force the AI layer to exist only as route handlers.



20. AI feedback loops

Support future feedback capture:


owner accepted suggestion

owner rejected suggestion

owner marked suggestion helpful or not helpful

remediation succeeded or failed

recommendation led to measurable positive outcome

tenant accepted policy suggestion

tenant dismissed policy suggestion

extracted rule confirmed or corrected by tenant


Store enough data so future tuning can improve:

policies

thresholds

model choices

when and how the system asks contextual follow-up questions



21. Final AI expectation

By the end of implementation:


AI must already be architecturally central

AI calls must be standardized

AI logging must be real

observations, incidents, and knowledge structures must exist

ops intelligence must have a real place in the system

product intelligence must have a real place in the system

shared knowledge and memory must have a real place in the system

tenant knowledge capture must be progressive, not onboarding-heavy

future autonomy must have a safe policy model to build on


Do not build this as a vague future dream.


Build the architecture so the next stages of AI capability — stronger recommendations, richer memory, deeper business understanding, safer automations, and eventually more autonomous assistance — can grow cleanly from this foundation.

First: What kind of platform payments are we talking about?

You process and monetize transactions.

Take booking fee

Hold deposits

Payout businesses

Revenue share

Instant payout fees

That third layer can become huge.

Well you’ve mentioned 3 layers that payment sits across. Let’s explore them in detail 

Great — this is a major architecture decision, not just a “take card payments” feature. I’ll break the three layers down as separate business engines, then show how they connect into one platform model.

Thought for 15s
Yes — let’s break the 3 payment layers properly.

1. Customer → Business payments
This is money customers pay for appointments.

Use cases

Pay at venue, but booking is reserved.

Deposit to secure slot.

Full prepayment.

Cancellation/no-show fee.

Tips.

Gift cards / wallet credit later.

Membership-included bookings later.

Best launch approach
Start with optional deposits and pay at venue. Full prepay can come next. Deposits solve a real pain point without making adoption too heavy.

Rules needed

Business chooses: no deposit / fixed deposit / percentage deposit / full prepay.

Deposit can vary by service, customer risk, peak slot, or first-time customer.

Refund policy must be shown before payment.

Booking only becomes confirmed after payment succeeds, where deposit is required.

Payment failure means slot is not held or is held briefly with expiry.

2. Business → Platform payments
This is how businesses pay you for using the system.

Use cases

Monthly subscription.

Staff-seat pricing.

Website/storefront add-on.

AI assistant add-on.

Messaging credits.

Custom domain fee.

Premium analytics.

Higher booking volume tiers.

Recommended pricing structure

Starter: booking page + basic dashboard.

Pro: full booking + reminders + smart storefront.

Premium: AI concierge + advanced customer intelligence + no-show tools.

Add-ons: custom domain, extra staff, extra AI usage, messaging credits.

Stripe Billing is built for subscriptions, recurring payments, invoices, trials, plan changes, and usage-based billing. 

3. Platform-controlled financial flows
This is the deeper marketplace/fintech layer.

Use cases

Take a platform fee from each booking.

Split payments between business and platform.

Payout businesses.

Track refunds.

Track disputes/chargebacks.

Enable instant payout fees later.

Eventually offer cashflow advances or lending-like products.

Best long-term model
Use a connected-account model where each business has its own payment account, and your platform takes an application/platform fee. Stripe Connect supports connected accounts, destination charges, and application fees for platform-style money movement. 

This avoids you manually collecting all customer money into your own account and then paying businesses out, which can create heavier regulatory and operational risk.

How the layers connect
Example: haircut costs €30, deposit required is €10.

Customer pays €10 deposit.

Platform fee: maybe €0.50 or 5%.

Business receives the rest via connected account.

Booking is marked confirmed.

Notification is sent.

Payment event is logged.

AI can later learn that deposits reduce no-shows for certain services/times.

Then separately, the business pays you €39/month for Pro.

So you earn from:

SaaS subscription

transaction fees

premium website/storefront tools

AI add-ons

messaging credits

Prompt 7 — Payments, Billing, Deposits, Marketplace Money Movement, and Revenue Architecture
Use this as the dedicated payment prompt.

# Prompt 7 — Payments, Billing, Deposits, Marketplace Money Movement, and Revenue Architecture

Build the payment layer as a first-class platform capability, not as a simple checkout add-on.

This platform has three payment layers:

1. Customer-to-business payments
2. Business-to-platform payments
3. Platform-controlled financial flows

Payments must integrate with bookings, subscriptions, storefronts, notifications, AI, reporting, refunds, disputes, payouts, and future financial intelligence.

The platform should be designed so payments can start simple but scale into a serious revenue engine.

Use Stripe as the default recommended provider unless there is a strong reason not to.

---

## 1. Payment philosophy

Payments must support the platform’s long-term position as an operating system for appointment-based service businesses.

The payment system should support:
- pay at venue
- booking deposits
- full prepayment
- tips
- cancellation fees
- no-show fees
- memberships later
- prepaid bundles later
- gift cards later
- platform subscriptions
- usage billing
- add-on billing
- connected business accounts
- platform fees
- payouts
- refunds
- disputes
- reconciliation
- AI-powered payment recommendations later

Payments should be modular, auditable, safe, and deeply connected to the booking lifecycle.

---

## 2. Customer-to-business payments

Customer payments are payments made by customers for services.

Supported modes:

### Pay at venue
Customer books now and pays in person.

Use case:
- easiest adoption
- useful for businesses not ready for online payments
- booking can still be confirmed without payment

### Deposit required
Customer pays a deposit to secure booking.

Use case:
- no-show reduction
- peak-time protection
- first-time customer protection
- high-value services

Deposit types:
- fixed amount
- percentage of service price
- service-specific deposit
- staff-specific deposit later
- customer-risk-based deposit later
- peak-time deposit later

### Full prepayment
Customer pays full amount before appointment.

Use case:
- tutors
- premium salons
- high-value appointments
- limited availability services

### Tips
Optional customer tips after or during booking.

### Cancellation / no-show charges
Only if policy is clearly shown and customer consents.

For MVP, do not overbuild risky automatic charges. Support policy-aware design and payment record structures.

---

## 3. Booking/payment lifecycle

Booking and payment state must be linked clearly.

If no payment is required:
- booking may move directly to CONFIRMED

If deposit is required:
- booking may start as PENDING_PAYMENT
- payment is created
- customer completes payment
- payment success confirms booking
- payment failure releases or expires the slot

If full prepayment is required:
- booking confirms only after successful full payment

Required behavior:
- do not confirm paid-required bookings before payment success
- do not double-book while payment is pending
- support short slot hold expiry later
- log all payment attempts
- make booking failure/payment failure understandable to the customer

Consider adding booking/payment statuses such as:
- PAYMENT_NOT_REQUIRED
- PAYMENT_PENDING
- PARTIALLY_PAID
- PAID
- PAYMENT_FAILED
- REFUNDED
- PARTIALLY_REFUNDED

If adding these directly to Booking is messy, create separate Payment records and compute payment state from them.

---

## 4. Business-to-platform payments

Businesses pay the platform through SaaS billing.

Support subscription architecture for:
- Free / Starter
- Pro
- Premium
- Enterprise later

Potential pricing levers:
- monthly subscription
- annual subscription
- staff seat count
- booking volume limits
- website/storefront add-on
- custom domain add-on
- AI assistant add-on
- messaging credits
- advanced analytics add-on
- payment processing add-on
- reduced platform fee on higher plans

Use Stripe Billing or equivalent architecture.

Business billing features:
- plan selection
- trial period
- upgrade/downgrade
- cancellation
- failed subscription payment handling
- invoices
- billing portal link
- subscription status enforcement
- plan feature gating

---

## 5. Platform-controlled money movement

The platform should eventually support marketplace-style money flows.

Recommended model:
- each business connects its own Stripe account
- platform takes an application/platform fee
- business receives payouts through its connected account

Avoid initially collecting all customer funds centrally and manually paying businesses unless legally and operationally prepared.

Support:
- connected payment account onboarding
- payment account status
- payout status
- platform fee tracking
- refund tracking
- dispute tracking
- reconciliation records

---

## 6. Payment provider strategy

Default provider:
- Stripe

Required Stripe concepts:
- PaymentIntent
- Checkout Session or embedded checkout
- Stripe Connect
- Connected Accounts
- Application Fees
- Webhooks
- Stripe Billing
- Customer objects
- Subscriptions
- Invoices
- Refunds
- Disputes
- Payouts

Provider abstraction:
Do not scatter Stripe calls across the app.

Create payment provider service layer:
- `src/services/payments/paymentProvider.ts`
- `src/services/payments/stripeProvider.ts`
- `src/services/payments/paymentService.ts`
- `src/services/billing/billingService.ts`
- `src/services/payouts/payoutService.ts`

All payment actions must pass through services.

---

## 7. Payment schema additions

Add or prepare these models.

### PaymentAccount
Represents the business’s connected payment account.

Fields:
- id
- businessId
- provider
- providerAccountId
- status
- chargesEnabled
- payoutsEnabled
- detailsSubmitted
- onboardingUrl nullable
- metadata Json nullable
- createdAt
- updatedAt

### PaymentIntentRecord
Represents an attempted payment linked to booking or platform billing.

Fields:
- id
- businessId nullable
- customerId nullable
- bookingId nullable
- provider
- providerPaymentIntentId nullable
- amountMinor
- currency
- status
- paymentPurpose
- checkoutUrl nullable
- metadata Json nullable
- createdAt
- updatedAt

PaymentPurpose enum:
- BOOKING_DEPOSIT
- BOOKING_FULL_PAYMENT
- TIP
- CANCELLATION_FEE
- NO_SHOW_FEE
- SUBSCRIPTION
- MEMBERSHIP
- GIFT_CARD
- CREDIT_TOPUP

### Payment
Represents completed or failed payment result.

Fields:
- id
- businessId nullable
- customerId nullable

- providerChargeId nullable

- platformFeeMinor nullable

- status

- metadata Json nullable
- createdAt
- updatedAt

### Refund
Fields:
- id
- businessId

- status
- createdAt
- updatedAt

### Payout
Fields:
- id
- businessId

- createdAt
- updatedAt

### PlatformFee
Fields:
- id

- amountMinor
- currency
- feeType
- createdAt

FeeType enum:
- BOOKING_TRANSACTION

- INSTANT_PAYOUT
- CUSTOM_DOMAIN

### SubscriptionPlan
Fields:
- id
- key
- name

- updatedAt

### BusinessSubscription
Fields:

### Invoice
Fields:

- amountDueMinor
- amountPaidMinor
- currency

- paidAt nullable

- updatedAt

### PaymentPolicy
Represents business-level payment rules.

Fields:
- id
- businessId
- defaultPaymentMode
- defaultDepositType nullable
- defaultDepositValue nullable
- cancellationPolicyText nullable
- refundPolicyText nullable
- noShowPolicyText nullable
- requireDepositForFirstTimeCustomers boolean
- requireDepositForHighRiskCustomers boolean
- createdAt
- updatedAt

DefaultPaymentMode enum:
- PAY_AT_VENUE
- DEPOSIT_REQUIRED
- FULL_PREPAYMENT

---

## 8. Payment events

Emit events for:
- PAYMENT_ACCOUNT_CREATED
- PAYMENT_ACCOUNT_ONBOARDING_STARTED
- PAYMENT_ACCOUNT_ENABLED
- PAYMENT_REQUIRED
- PAYMENT_STARTED
- PAYMENT_SUCCEEDED
- PAYMENT_FAILED
- DEPOSIT_PAID
- FULL_PAYMENT_PAID
- REFUND_REQUESTED
- REFUND_SUCCEEDED
- REFUND_FAILED
- PAYOUT_CREATED
- PAYOUT_FAILED
- SUBSCRIPTION_STARTED
- SUBSCRIPTION_UPDATED
- SUBSCRIPTION_CANCELLED
- INVOICE_CREATED
- INVOICE_PAID
- INVOICE_PAYMENT_FAILED
- PLATFORM_FEE_CREATED
- PAYMENT_WEBHOOK_RECEIVED
- PAYMENT_WEBHOOK_FAILED

All payment events must be visible to the AI/ops layer.

---

## 9. Payment API routes

Implement or prepare routes for:

### Business payment account
- create/connect payment account
- get payment account status
- refresh onboarding link
- handle provider onboarding callback

### Customer payments
- create booking payment intent
- create deposit checkout
- create full prepayment checkout
- confirm payment status
- handle payment webhook

### Refunds
- create refund
- list refunds for booking/payment

### Business billing
- list plans
- create subscription checkout
- get current subscription
- open billing portal
- handle subscription webhook

### Payouts
- list payouts
- get payout detail

---

## 10. Payment UX requirements

### Customer checkout
Customer must clearly see:
- service
- business
- date/time
- staff if selected
- deposit/full amount
- remaining balance if deposit
- cancellation/refund policy
- secure checkout CTA

### Booking confirmation
After payment:
- show success
- show paid amount
- show remaining amount if applicable
- show receipt/payment reference where useful

### Business dashboard
Include:
- payment status on bookings
- earnings summary
- deposits collected
- payments failed
- refunds
- payout status later
- subscription/billing page

### Settings
Add payment settings:
- payment mode
- deposit rules
- refund policy
- cancellation policy
- connected account status
- subscription plan

---

## 11. AI and payments

AI must be able to use payment-related signals later.

Support:
- no-show/deposit recommendation
- failed payment anomaly detection
- refund pattern detection
- revenue trend summaries
- high-value customer detection
- churn warning for businesses
- payout failure summaries
- payment provider incident summaries

AI must not:
- change prices automatically
- issue refunds automatically
- charge customers automatically without explicit policy and consent
- enforce risky payment rules silently

---

## 12. Compliance and safety

Payment handling must be cautious.

Rules:
- do not store raw card details
- rely on provider-hosted or provider-secure checkout
- log provider IDs, not sensitive card data
- respect refunds and cancellation policies
- make customer consent clear
- keep audit trail for disputes
- do not fake financial success states
- verify payment webhooks securely

---

## 13. Testing requirements

Test:
- deposit-required booking flow
- pay-at-venue booking flow
- full-prepayment booking flow
- payment success webhook
- payment failure webhook
- booking confirmation after payment success
- booking not confirmed after payment failure
- valid refund creation path
- subscription status gating
- platform fee calculation where implemented

---

## 14. Seed data

Seed:
- business with pay-at-venue policy
- business with deposit policy
- bookings with no payment required
- bookings with deposit paid
- bookings with failed payment
- subscription plan examples
- business subscription example
- payment account example
- refund example
- platform fee example

---

## 15. Final payment expectation

By the end of implementation, payments must feel like a real platform capability.

Even if live Stripe credentials are not present, the structure must be complete enough that adding credentials and enabling production flows is straightforward.

Do not leave payments as vague future work.
Prompt 8 — Growth Sites, Storefronts, Discovery, CRM, Retention, and Commerce Expansion
# Prompt 8 — Growth Sites, Storefronts, Discovery, CRM, Retention, and Commerce Expansion

Build the growth layer of the platform.

This layer turns each business from “a booking account” into a digital storefront with booking, services, staff, contact, payments, customer capture, and future AI growth tools.

The goal is to create an additional revenue stream and a stronger acquisition/retention engine for the platform.

Do not treat this as a generic website builder like Wix. Treat it as a template-driven business storefront system designed specifically for appointment-based service businesses.

---

## 1. Growth layer mission

The growth layer should help businesses:
- look professional online
- get discovered
- convert visitors into bookings
- showcase services and staff
- collect leads
- sell memberships/gift cards later
- reduce dependency on Instagram-only presence
- connect website traffic directly into booking and customer CRM

This should become a monetizable product layer:
- basic booking page included
- smart storefront as paid add-on or higher-tier feature
- custom domain as paid add-on
- AI-generated copy as premium feature
- SEO and growth analytics as premium feature

---

## 2. Storefront concept

Each business should be able to have a public web presence.

Supported URL models:
- platform path: `/b/{businessSlug}`
- storefront path: `/s/{businessSlug}` or equivalent
- subdomain later: `{businessSlug}.platform.com`
- custom domain later: `www.businessname.com`

The storefront should be powered by the same business, service, staff, availability, booking, payment, and customer data already in the platform.

Do not duplicate data unnecessarily.

---

## 3. Storefront page types

Support these page types.

### Home page
Includes:
- business name
- hero section
- cover image
- logo
- short intro
- primary CTA: Book Now
- secondary CTA: Message / WhatsApp / Instagram
- featured services
- featured staff
- reviews/testimonials later
- location/contact
- opening hours

### Services page
Includes:
- service categories
- service cards
- price
- duration
- description
- book button for each service

### Staff page
Includes:
- staff cards
- photo
- bio
- specialties
- book with staff CTA

### Gallery page
Includes:
- images of work
- categories
- captions
- optional Instagram import later

### Contact page
Includes:
- address
- phone
- email
- map placeholder or map integration later
- social buttons
- opening hours

### Policies page
Includes:
- cancellation policy
- refund policy
- deposit policy
- lateness policy
- no-show policy

### Memberships / Offers page later
Includes:
- prepaid packages
- membership plans
- gift cards
- seasonal offers

---

## 4. Template strategy

Start template-driven, not freeform drag-and-drop.

Initial templates:
- Classic
- Modern
- Luxury
- Bold

Each template should be configurable by:
- logo
- cover image
- primary color
- accent color
- font style if practical
- section visibility
- service display style
- staff display style
- CTA style

Do not build a complex page builder at MVP unless necessary.

---

The storefront must be fast, clean, mobile-first, and conversion-oriented.

Suggested modules:

- `src/ai/product/offerSuggestion.ts`

- service-specific landing pages
- discovery marketplace pages

Do not overbuild marketplace discovery immediately, but keep structure ready.

Future discovery layer may include:
- search by location
- search by service category

- ratings/reviews

- map view
- “available today” filter
- promoted listings

Do not build full discovery marketplace now unless explicitly asked, but architecture should not block it.

## 12. CRM and retention growth features

Storefronts should feed into CRM.

Capture:
- leads

- referral links

## 13. Commerce expansion

The storefront should later sell:
- memberships
- prepaid bundles
- gift cards
- service packages
- event slots
- product add-ons if relevant

Do not build all commerce features now, but design storefront and payment integration so they fit naturally.

## 17. Integration with payments

Storefronts must integrate with:
- deposits
- full prepayment
- memberships later
- gift cards later
- paid offers later

A customer should be able to:
- browse service
- click book
- choose slot
- pay deposit/full amount if required
- receive confirmation

---

## 18. Integration with AI

AI should learn from storefront performance.

Potential insights:
- high traffic but low bookings
- service page abandonment
- weak service descriptions
- missed rebooking opportunities
- popular services deserving featured placement
- suggested offers for low-demand days

AI must surface recommendations, not silently rewrite storefront content.

Build it as an integrated growth engine for service businesses.

This product = operations-first (bookings, storefronts, customers, messaging)

Those are different entry points.

If you force them together too early at the brand level, you’ll confuse users and slow adoption.

The Right Mental Model
Don’t think:

“Where does this sit in the ecosystem hierarchy?”

5. Clean Technical Approach (Very Important)
Create a Provider Pattern Now
Example:
/src/services/payments/
  ├── paymentService.ts
  ├── providers/
  │     ├── stripeProvider.ts
  │     ├── mulahProvider.ts (empty for now)
paymentService.ts
Handles:

createPayment

confirmPayment

refund

getStatus

stripeProvider.ts
Actual implementation (for now)

mulahProvider.ts
Stub:

export const mulahProvider = {
  createPayment: async () => {
    throw new Error("Mulah not implemented yet")
  }
}
This is CRUCIAL
Without this:

You’ll have to rewrite your entire payment system later.

With this:

You swap providers cleanly.

Not just payments.

Also:

Subscriptions
Wallet
Financial analytics
All go through:

financialService

Good — Replit is actually fine for this stage if you use it correctly. The mistake people make is treating it as a toy or, on the flip side, trying to force it into full production too early.

Let’s be precise about how you should use it.

1. Position Replit Properly in Your Stack
Use Replit as:

Development + rapid iteration + AI-assisted build environment

Not as:

long-term production infrastructure (at least not initially)

2. What Replit Is Excellent For (your use case)
For Bliq, Replit gives you:

fast setup (no local env headache)

built-in hosting for testing

AI coding assistance

easy sharing/testing links

quick iteration cycles

This is perfect for:

Phase 0 → Phase 3 (MVP build)
booking engine

dashboard

basic payments

public booking page

3. What You Should NOT Rely on Replit For (yet)
Avoid using Replit as:

your final production DB

your long-term backend infra

your scaling layer

your payment webhook reliability layer

Those need stronger guarantees later.

4. Recommended Architecture (Even on Replit)
Frontend + Backend
Next.js (App Router)

Hosted on Replit

Database
Use external DB from day 1

Recommended:

Supabase (Postgres)

Do NOT rely on:

in-memory

local file DB

Replit-only DB

Payments
Stripe (via abstraction layer we discussed)

Storage (later)
Supabase storage or S3-compatible

5. Replit Project Structure (Important)
Set it up cleanly from the start:

/app
  /dashboard
  /booking
  /public

/src
  /lib
  /services
    /payments
    /booking
    /customers
  /db
  /utils

/prisma
  schema.prisma
6. Critical Rule While Using Replit
Do not let Replit dictate your architecture

You still build like you’re deploying to:

Vercel

AWS later

Replit is just the environment, not the system design.

Objective

1. Prerequisites (do this first)
Install:

Node.js (v18+)

npm or pnpm (pnpm preferred)

Install Cursor:
→ Cursor

Create accounts:

Supabase

Stripe

2. Create Project
In terminal:

npx create-next-app@latest bliq
Select:
TypeScript → Yes

App Router → Yes

Tailwind → Yes

ESLint → Yes

src/ directory → Yes

Import alias → Yes

Enter project
cd bliq
3. Install Core Dependencies
npm install prisma @prisma/client
npm install zod date-fns
npm install stripe
npm install axios
Dev:

npm install -D ts-node
4. Initialize Prisma
npx prisma init
This creates:

/prisma/schema.prisma
.env
5. Setup Supabase Database
Go to Supabase:

Create new project

Go to Settings → Database

Copy connection string

Update .env:

DATABASE_URL="your_supabase_connection_string"
6. First Prisma Schema (Core Only)
Replace schema.prisma with:

generator client {
  provider = "prisma-client-js"
}

datasource db {
  provider = "postgresql"
  url      = env("DATABASE_URL")
}

model Business {
  id        String   @id @default(cuid())
  name      String
  slug      String   @unique
  createdAt DateTime @default(now())

  services  Service[]
  staff     Staff[]
  bookings  Booking[]
}

model Service {
  id          String   @id @default(cuid())
  name        String
  duration    Int
  price       Int
  businessId  String

  business    Business @relation(fields: [businessId], references: [id])
}

model Staff {
  id         String   @id @default(cuid())
  name       String
  businessId String

  business   Business @relation(fields: [businessId], references: [id])
}

model Booking {
  id         String   @id @default(cuid())
  businessId String
  serviceId  String
  staffId    String?
  customerName String
  customerPhone String
  startTime  DateTime
  endTime    DateTime
  status     String   @default("pending")

  business   Business @relation(fields: [businessId], references: [id])
  service    Service  @relation(fields: [serviceId], references: [id])
}
7. Run Migration
npx prisma migrate dev --name init
Then:

npx prisma generate
8. Setup Prisma Client
Create:

/src/lib/db.ts
import { PrismaClient } from '@prisma/client'

const globalForPrisma = global as unknown as { prisma: PrismaClient }

export const db =
  globalForPrisma.prisma ||
  new PrismaClient()

if (process.env.NODE_ENV !== 'production') globalForPrisma.prisma = db
9. Setup Base Folder Architecture
Create structure:

/src
  /app
  /lib
  /services
    /booking
    /payments
    /customers
  /utils
10. Create Health Check Route
/src/app/api/health/route.ts
import { NextResponse } from 'next/server'

export async function GET() {
  return NextResponse.json({ status: 'ok' })
}
Run:

npm run dev
Visit:

http://localhost:3000/api/health
You should see:

{ "status": "ok" }
11. Test Database Connection
Create:

/src/app/api/test-db/route.ts
import { NextResponse } from 'next/server'
import { db } from '@/lib/db'

export async function GET() {
  const businesses = await db.business.findMany()

  return NextResponse.json({
    success: true,
    count: businesses.length
  })
}

1. ALL logic must live in /src/services/*
2. API routes must remain thin
3. Multi-tenancy enforced via businessId
4. No duplication

6. Code must be production-quality (no placeholders)

## 📦 NEW DATA MODEL

Add a new model:

BusinessHours:
- id
- businessId
- dayOfWeek (0 = Sunday, 6 = Saturday)
- openTime (string, e.g. "09:00")
- closeTime (string, e.g. "18:00")

Each business can have multiple entries (one per day).

---

## 🧠 REQUIRED MODULES

---

### 1. availabilityService.ts

Responsibilities:
- Manage business hours

Functions:

setBusinessHours({
  businessId,
  dayOfWeek,
  openTime,
  closeTime
})

getBusinessHours(businessId)

Rules:
- Upsert per day (one entry per day)
- Validate openTime < closeTime

---

### 2. slotService.ts

This is the core of Phase 2.

Function:

getAvailableSlots({
  businessId,
  serviceId,
  date
})

Where:
- date = YYYY-MM-DD

---

### SLOT GENERATION LOGIC

Steps:

1. Fetch business hours for that day
2. If no hours → return empty array

3. Fetch service (get duration)

4. Generate time slots:
   - Start at openTime
   - End before closeTime
   - Increment in 15-minute intervals
   - Each slot must fit full service duration

5. Fetch existing bookings for that business on that date

6. Filter out conflicting slots:
   - A slot is invalid if it overlaps with any booking

Overlap logic:
slotStart < bookingEnd AND slotEnd > bookingStart

---

### OUTPUT FORMAT

Return:

[
  {
    startTime: Date,
    endTime: Date
  }
]

---

### 3. UPDATE bookingService.ts

Modify createBooking:

Before creating:
- Check slot availability using slotService
- Reject if slot is invalid or already booked

---

Paste this into Cursor first, before asking it to build anything:

You are working on a product called Bliq.

Bliq is a production-minded, AI-native business operating platform for service businesses. It is not only a booking app and it is not only for barbers. Barbers may be the first launch niche, but the platform must be architected generically for any appointment-based or service-based business: barbers, salons, nail techs, lash techs, tutors, personal trainers, tattoo artists, mechanics, home services, mobile beauty providers, consultants, and similar businesses.

Bliq’s core idea:

Businesses should be able to accept bookings, manage customers, handle payments, create a public storefront, communicate through modern messaging channels, and eventually use AI to reduce manual operational work.

The long-term product vision is:

Bliq is the operating system for service businesses.

Core platform pillars:

1. Booking and scheduling
- business setup
- services
- staff
- availability
- time off
- slot generation
- booking creation
- booking status management
- booking history

2. Customers and identity
- customer profiles
- customer booking history
- notes
- channel identities
- future identity merging across WhatsApp, SMS, Instagram, Snapchat, web, and app

3. Payments
- pay at venue
- deposits
- full prepayment
- platform subscriptions
- platform fees
- payment abstraction layer
- Stripe first, future Mulah provider later
- do not hardcode Stripe directly into business logic

4. Storefronts and growth
- public booking page
- business profile page
- template-driven storefronts
- services page
- staff page
- gallery
- contact page
- future custom domains
- future SEO and discovery

5. Messaging
- future WhatsApp Cloud API
- Instagram DM integration
- SMS integration
- Snapchat login/identity
- inbound and outbound message logs
- messaging channels should link to customer identity and booking flow

6. AI-native architecture
AI is not just a chatbot. AI is intended to support:
- customer insights
- booking recommendations
- no-show risk
- rebooking suggestions
- operational monitoring
- incident summarization
- knowledge capture
- future low-risk automation

AI must be built safely:
- AI must not be the source of truth for booking validation
- deterministic backend logic must validate bookings, payments, and permissions
- AI outputs must be logged
- AI actions must be auditable
- dangerous actions must require approval

7. Platform operations
The platform must eventually include:
- admin control dashboard
- feature flags
- kill switches
- incidents
- remediation actions
- audit logs
- support tooling
- analytics
- monitoring
- alerting

Technical direction:

Use:
- Next.js with App Router
- TypeScript everywhere
- Tailwind CSS
- Prisma ORM
- PostgreSQL via Supabase
- Stripe through a payment abstraction layer
- Capacitor later for iOS and Android wrappers
- Sentry later for monitoring
- OpenAI later through a centralized AI client wrapper

Architecture rules:

1. Use service-layer architecture.
Business logic must live in src/services, not inside UI components or API routes.

2. API routes must be thin.
Routes should validate requests, call services, and return responses.

3. Multi-tenancy must be respected.
Most records must be scoped by businessId. Never write queries that could leak data between businesses.

4. Keep naming generic.
Do not hardcode barber-only concepts. Use Business, Staff, Service, Customer, Booking, AvailabilityRule, ChannelIdentity, Payment, Storefront, Event, etc.

5. Build for future expansion.
The first build may start with core bookings, but the structure must not block payments, messaging, storefronts, AI, analytics, support, or automation.

6. Payment provider abstraction is required.
Use paymentService as the public interface. Stripe is the first implementation. Mulah may become a future provider, but Mulah is not built yet.

7. AI provider abstraction is required.
Use a central AI client later. No random direct AI calls scattered across the codebase.

8. Events are important.
Important actions should eventually emit structured events, such as BOOKING_CREATED, PAYMENT_SUCCEEDED, CUSTOMER_CREATED, AI_OBSERVATION_CREATED, etc.

Current build priority:

Start with the smallest serious foundation:
1. project foundation
2. database schema
3. business onboarding
4. services
5. staff
6. availability
7. slot generation
8. booking creation
9. public booking page
10. dashboard
11. payment deposits
12. notifications
13. storefronts
14. messaging integrations
15. AI/ops intelligence

When generating code:
- do not create toy/demo architecture
- do not put business logic in pages
- do not duplicate logic
- do not skip validation
- do not hardcode one business type
- do not create vague placeholder files unless explicitly asked
- keep code clean, modular, typed, and understandable for a solo founder

The immediate goal is to build Bliq as a strong, scalable foundation that can become a full operating platform for service businesses.
Then ask Cursor:

Here’s what was added and how to use it.

Files touched
prisma/schema.prisma — full domain model (enums + models + indexes + onDelete behavior).

npx prisma validate and npx prisma generate succeed. npx prisma migrate deploy failed here only because Postgres at localhost:5432 was unreachable (expected in this environment).

Model relationships (mental model)
Tenancy hub: Business is the tenant root. Almost every operational/financial row includes businessId for scoping and indexing.

Identity & access

User — login identity (email unique).
BusinessMembership — many-to-many User ↔ Business with MembershipRole (OWNER … VIEWER). One row per user per business (@@unique([userId, businessId])).
Staff — operational person at a business; optional userId to link the same human to User. StaffRole is an on-the-job hat (PROVIDER, COORDINATOR, OTHER), separate from membership roles (product vs org access).
Catalog & assignment

Service — bookable offering under a business (durationMinutes, optional basePriceMinorUnits + currency, metadata for future pricing rules).
StaffServiceAssignment — which staff can perform which service; businessId duplicated for tenant filters/indexes; @@unique([staffId, serviceId]) so a staff–service pair is assigned once.
Customers & channels

Customer — tenant-scoped customer record.
ChannelIdentity — how you reach someone (ChannelType + value), under businessId; optional customerId until linked. @@unique([businessId, channel, value]) avoids duplicate channel rows per tenant.
Scheduling

Booking — businessId, customerId, serviceId, optional staffId, startsAt / endsAt, BookingStatus, optional notes + metadata (e.g. future resources, recurrence ids).
AvailabilityRule — per-staffId, weekly window: weekday (0 = Sunday … 6 = Saturday), startMinutes / endMinutes from local midnight, timezone, optional effective range + metadata (e.g. future location-specific rules).
TimeOff — blocked intervals for a staff member under the business.
Observability

Event — append-only style log: level (EventLevel), type, optional subjectType / subjectId, payload, optional actorUserId → User. businessId is optional so you can record platform-level events; tenant events should always set businessId.
Payments

PaymentIntentRecord — provider-agnostic intent (provider default "stripe", optional externalId, PaymentIntentStatus, amounts in minor units, optional bookingId). No DB unique on (provider, externalId) so you can have rows before the provider assigns an id; enforce uniqueness in the app when externalId is set.
Payment — ledger-style row: PaymentStatus, PaymentPurpose, optional links to bookingId and paymentIntentRecordId.
PaymentAccount — payout / Connect-style account stub per business (externalAccountId, PaymentAccountStatus, capabilities / metadata JSON).
Configuration

FeatureFlag — per business only (@@unique([businessId, key])). Global or cross-tenant flags stay out of this table (env, separate system, or a later migration).
Extensibility without storefront/AI/support tables: optional Json settings / metadata / capabilities / payload on several models so you can attach IDs, experiments, or integration payloads without schema churn.

Indexes (high level)
Tenant sweep: @@index([businessId]) (or composite with domain keys) on tenant-owned tables.
Booking: [businessId, startsAt], [staffId, startsAt], plus customerId, serviceId, staffId.
TimeOff: [businessId, staffId, startsAt], [staffId, startsAt].
Event: [businessId, occurredAt], [level, occurredAt], [type, occurredAt].
Payment* / intents: businessId, status composites where useful, bookingId where linked.
Commands to run
Local dev (creates/updates DB from migrations, interactive name if you add new ones):

npx prisma migrate dev
For a new clone that already has this migration folder checked in, either:

npx prisma migrate dev
or, in CI / production-style:

npx prisma migrate deploy
After schema changes:

npx prisma generate
Note: migrate dev will apply pending migrations and record them in _prisma_migrations. If this migration was never applied on your machine, migrate dev or migrate deploy will create all enums and tables from 20260501220000_core_domain/migration.sql. If you already had tables from an older empty schema, resolve drift first (backup, then reset or repair) before applying.

Still blocked by connectivity, but this is now a Supabase connection string/network issue, not a Bliq code issue.

Try these in order:

1. Fix the Supabase pooler host
Your URL shows:

aws-0-eu-west-1.pooler.supabase.com:6543
Supabase pooler hosts usually include the project ref, often like:

aws-0-eu-west-1.pooler.supabase.com
but the username usually must be:

postgres.YOUR_PROJECT_REF
So your URL should look like:

DATABASE_URL="postgresql://postgres.YOUR_PROJECT_REF:YOUR_PASSWORD@aws-0-eu-west-1.pooler.supabase.com:6543/postgres?pgbouncer=true&connection_limit=1"
Make sure you copied the Transaction pooler string exactly from Supabase, not manually edited.

2. URL-encode your password
If your DB password has characters like:

@ # % / ? : & =
the URL breaks unless encoded.

Example:

p@ssword
must become:

p%40ssword
3. Test network access to port 6543
Run this in PowerShell:

Test-NetConnection aws-0-eu-west-1.pooler.supabase.com -Port 6543
If TcpTestSucceeded is False, your network/VPN/firewall is blocking it.

4. Try Supabase Session pooler or Direct URL
In Supabase, copy each connection option exactly and test:

# Session pooler may be 5432 or 6543 depending Supabase display
DATABASE_URL="..."

# Direct connection usually:
DATABASE_URL="postgresql://postgres:YOUR_PASSWORD@db.YOUR_PROJECT_REF.supabase.co:5432/postgres"
Then run:

npx prisma migrate dev

This screenshot explains your exact issue.

🔴 Root cause (finally clear)
Supabase says:

Direct connection endpoint = IPv6 only

Your machine = likely IPv4 only network

So:

❌ Direct DB URL → won’t work (IPv6 required)

❌ Dedicated pooler → also IPv6 unless you pay

✅ Shared pooler (port 6543) → supports IPv4 (FREE)

🧠 Why Prisma is hanging
Even though your port test passed, Prisma migrations + PgBouncer (Supabase pooler) can behave weirdly because:

Prisma migrations are NOT designed for pooled connections

That’s the key problem.

✅ Correct setup (this is the fix)
You need DUAL URLs

1. Use pooler for runtime
2. Use direct for migrations
BUT since direct = IPv6 only…

👉 You must force Prisma to use pooler for now (with correct flags)

🔧 Fix your .env
Use EXACTLY this format:

DATABASE_URL="postgresql://postgres.YOUR_PROJECT_REF:YOUR_URL_ENCODED_PASSWORD@aws-0-eu-west-1.pooler.supabase.com:6543/postgres?pgbouncer=true&connection_limit=1"
Critical:
username = postgres.YOUR_PROJECT_REF

password = URL encoded

include pgbouncer=true

include connection_limit=1

Ctrl + C
2. In Supabase, get the Session pooler URL
Go to:

Supabase → Project Settings → Database → Connection string

Pick:

Session pooler
Not transaction pooler.

It may use port 5432 or sometimes 6543, but the key is Session pooler, not transaction.

3. Temporarily set .env
For now, set:

DATABASE_URL="SESSION_POOLER_URL_HERE"
Make sure username format is like:

postgres.YOUR_PROJECT_REF
and password is URL-encoded.

4. Run
npx prisma db push
npx prisma generate
npm run build
5. Later runtime setup
Once schema is pushed successfully, you can either keep session pooler for local dev or later split:

DATABASE_URL="transaction/session pooler runtime URL"
DIRECT_URL="direct URL if available later"
But for now, simplest is:

Use Session pooler for Prisma + local dev.

Do not pay for IPv4 yet. Use the shared/session pooler first.


...and password is URL-encoded... so am i leaving it as [YOUR-PASSWORD] or as [john999!!]?

Good question — this is where people silently break their DB connection.

❌ Do NOT leave it as [YOUR-PASSWORD]
That’s just a placeholder.

✅ Use your actual password, e.g.:
john999!!
⚠️ But here’s the important part:
Because your password contains special characters (!), you must URL-encode it.

Example:
Raw password:

john999!!
Encoded:

john999%21%21
🔧 Final .env example
DATABASE_URL="postgresql://postgres.YOUR_PROJECT_REF:john999%21%21@aws-0-eu-west-1.pooler.supabase.com:5432/postgres"
🧠 Quick encoding guide (common chars)
Character	Encode
@	%40
!	%21
#	%23
/	%2F
:	%3A
🚀 Easiest way
Use this (quick and safe):

Google: “URL encode tool”

Paste your password

Copy result

✅ Then run
npx prisma db push
If it still hangs
Then it’s not password — it’s pooler type (we’ll fix that next).

Say “done” after you update .env and run the command, and we’ll move forward immediately.


what if its something like OludandeMi26!!

Good — let’s do it properly.

Your password:
OludandeMi26!!
URL-encoded:
OludandeMi26%21%21
Final .env example
DATABASE_URL="postgresql://postgres.YOUR_PROJECT_REF:OludandeMi26%21%21@aws-0-eu-west-1.pooler.supabase.com:5432/postgres"
Rule to remember (simple)
Letters & numbers → leave as is

Special characters → encode

In your case:

!! → %21%21

Quick sanity check
If your password was:

OludandeMi26!@#
It would become:

OludandeMi26%21%40%23

# Bliq Build Plan — Cursor Development Manual

## Product Identity

Bliq is a mobile-first, AI-native operating platform for service businesses.

It is not only a booking app and not only for barbers. Barbers may be the first test niche, but the platform must be generic enough for:

- barbers
- salons
- nail techs
- lash techs
- tutors
- personal trainers
- tattoo artists
- consultants
- mechanics
- mobile beauty providers
- home service businesses
- any appointment-based or service-based business

Bliq’s long-term vision:

> Bliq helps any service business run bookings, customers, payments, storefronts, messaging, automation, and intelligence from one platform.

The first build should focus on a serious foundation, not a toy MVP.

---

# Current Technical Direction

Use:

- Next.js App Router
- TypeScript
- Tailwind CSS
- Prisma ORM
- PostgreSQL via Supabase
- Zod for validation
- Stripe first via payment abstraction
- Future Mulah payment provider
- Capacitor later for iOS and Android
- Sentry later for monitoring
- Central AI client later for product/ops intelligence

Important current setup note:

- Repo uses `src/` structure.
- `@/*` points to `src/*`.
- `styles/`, `prisma/`, and `public/` remain at repo root.
- Prisma schema is synced with the database using `npx prisma db push`.
- Supabase session pooler is being used because direct connection is IPv6-only.

---

# Architecture Rules

Cursor must follow these rules at all times:

1. Business logic must live in `src/services/*`.
2. API routes must stay thin.
3. UI components must not contain business rules.
4. Every tenant-owned operation must be scoped by `businessId`.
5. Never hardcode barber-only concepts.
6. Use generic names: Business, Staff, Service, Customer, Booking, AvailabilityRule, Payment, Storefront, Event.
7. Use Zod validation on API input.
8. Use Prisma only through the shared Prisma client.
9. Important actions must emit events.
10. Do not introduce random providers directly into business logic.
11. Stripe must sit behind a payment abstraction.
12. Future AI must sit behind a central AI abstraction.
13. Do not build fake placeholder files unless the integration genuinely needs external credentials.
14. Every phase must compile before moving on.

---

Status: mostly complete.

- establish repo structure
- set up Prisma
- connect Supabase
- validate build

Expected files:
- `src/app`
- `src/lib`
- `src/services`
- `src/components`
- `src/types`
- `src/hooks`
- `src/utils`
- `prisma/schema.prisma`

Completion criteria:
- `npm run build` passes
- `npx prisma generate` passes
- database schema synced

Phase 4 — Slot Engine
Purpose:
Generate valid bookable appointment slots.

Build:

slot service

slot API route

slot conflict logic

Files:

src/services/booking/slotService.ts

src/app/api/slots/route.ts

Cursor prompt:

Implement Phase 4 of Bliq: Slot Engine.

Context:
Bliq needs deterministic available-slot generation. AI must never validate bookings. Slot generation must be backend-owned.

Build:
src/services/booking/slotService.ts

Function:
getAvailableSlots({
  businessId,
  serviceId,
  date,
  staffId?
})

Input:
- businessId
- serviceId
- date as YYYY-MM-DD
- optional staffId

Rules:
1. Load service and validate it belongs to business and is active.
2. Determine candidate staff:
   - if staffId provided, use that staff after validating it belongs to business and is active
   - if no staffId, use active staff assigned to the service
3. Load availability rules for candidate staff for that weekday.
4. Generate candidate slots using 15-minute interval.
5. Slot duration = service.durationMinutes.
6. Apply service buffers if schema supports them, otherwise leave clear TODO for buffer support.
7. Remove slots that overlap:
   - existing CONFIRMED or PENDING bookings
   - staff TimeOff
8. Do not return past slots.
9. Return slots grouped by staff or flattened with staffId included.

Conflict formula:
slotStart < existingEnd AND slotEnd > existingStart

Output shape:
[
  {
    staffId: string,
    startAt: string,
    endAt: string
  }
]

Create API:
GET /api/slots?businessId=...&serviceId=...&date=YYYY-MM-DD&staffId=optional

Rules:
- API route thin
- zod validation
- no UI
- no booking creation yet

Emit event:
SLOTS_REQUESTED with businessId, serviceId, date, optional staffId, resultCount

After implementation:
- run build/typecheck
- provide examples for testing
Completion criteria:

slots generated correctly

existing bookings block slots

time off blocks slots

inactive staff/services ignored

build passes

2. Booking service
File:
src/services/booking/bookingService.ts

Functions:
- createBooking({ businessId, serviceId, staffId, customerInput, channelType, startAt, notes? })

  PENDING -> CONFIRMED
  PENDING -> CANCELLED
  CONFIRMED -> COMPLETED
  CONFIRMED -> CANCELLED
  CONFIRMED -> NO_SHOW
- reject invalid transitions
